SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
Our borings generally encountered highly weathered materials residual soils and
saprolite consisting of medium stiff to very stiff clayey silts along the majority of the
proposed Phase 1A alignment In areas near stream crossings and flood plains we
encountered deposits of alluvium consisting of very soft to stiff silty clays and clayey silts
We also encountered variable amounts of organic materials in some of the borings near
the stream crossings In addition some borings encountered soft to medium stiff clayey
silt pockets in the residual soil layer
In general the alluvium residual soils and saprolite at the project site are
underlain by basalt formation at greater depths Due to the linear extent of the project
limits and variations in topographic and geologic features the depths to basalt formation
vary significantly along the highway alignment from about 20 to 57 feet below the
existing ground surface Based on our field exploration the basalt formations generally
range from extremely weathered to relatively unweathered and from soft to hard
Groundwater levels also varied significantly in our borings from near the existing
ground surface to depths of about 31 5 feet below the existing ground surface at the time
of our field exploration We did not encounter groundwater within the depths drilled in
14 of the 38 borings drilled within the Phase 1A project limits It should be noted that
groundwater levels likely will fluctuate depending on factors such as seasonal rainfall
presence of springs and or seepage storm surge conditions time of year and other
factors
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered along the proposed alignment
we believe cut and fill slopes may be designed with a slope inclination of 2H 1V or flatter
along the proposed alignment Where steeper cut slope inclinations are desired such as
the cut slopes along Hoomana Road steepened cut slopes with slope stabilization and
special erosion control measures may be incorporated into the design
Based on the information provided new box culvert installations will require
placement of about 12 to 18 feet of new embankment fills on top of the culverts to achieve
the design grades Because placement of new fills over soft ground conditions will cause
ground settlements we recommend over excavating the soft soils to expose firm
unyielding ground under the new box culvert crossings The over excavated soil should
be properly disposed off site and replaced with free draining granular fill prior to new
Hawaii California
SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
embankment fill placement and or pavement construction In general removal and
replacement should be implemented in the other areas with limited amounts of soft soil
deposits such as at the minor drainage crossings and at inverts of small pipe culverts
where appropriate
Ground settlements are anticipated when new fills are placed over soft ground
conditions and or areal fill is placed to raise the site to the proposed finished elevations In
order to reduce the effects of the anticipated settlements on the pavements structures
and other improvements constructed on the fills implementation of a settlement waiting
period will be necessary in selected locations after placement of the fills and prior to
construction of improvements on the fills
Our preliminary analyses indicate the settlement waiting period may range from a
minimum of 1 month to approximately 6 months depending on the fill thickness
foundation soils and implementation of soft soil stabilization measures Because it is
difficult to accurately predict the exact time required for the filled ground to settle we
recommend implementing a settlement monitoring program using settlement gauges and
settlement markers Therefore provisions should be made for potential delays in the
construction schedule where a settlement waiting period is required
A new three span bridge and additions to the existing Lihue Mill Bridge will be
constructed for the project Based on the subsurface conditions encountered in the
borings scour considerations and the anticipated structural load demands of the bridge
structure we recommend supporting the abutment and pier structures of the new bridge
and existing bridge additions by a deep foundation system consisting of cast in place
concrete drilled shafts The drilled shafts should have a diameter of 5 feet and extend to
depths ranging from about 65 to 105 feet below the bottoms of foundation caps A
compressive load capacity of 1 060 to 1 800 kips may be used for the design of drilled
shafts for the strength limit state Due to the relatively high compressive load capacities
of the drilled shafts a trial shaft and two sacrificial load test shafts should be installed
prior to construction of production drilled shafts
The text of this report should be referred to for detailed discussion and specific
recommendations for design of the highway widening project
END OF SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
Hawaii California

SECTION 1 0 GENERAL
1 1 Introduction
This report presents the results of our geotechnical engineering exploration and
engineering analyses performed for the proposed Kaumualii Highway Widening Phase 1A
project from the Vicinity of Anonui Street to Lihue on the Island of Kauai Hawaii The
project location and general vicinity are shown on the Project Location Map Plate 1
This report summarizes the findings and geotechnical recommendations based on
our field exploration laboratory testing and engineering analyses performed for the
proposed highway widening project The recommendations presented herein are intended
for the design of grading foundations soil stabilization retaining structures including
specialty retaining structures and pavements only The findings and recommendations
presented herein are subject to the limitations noted at the end of this report
A preliminary geotechnical engineering exploration was conducted in support of
the preliminary engineering for this project The preliminary geotechnical engineering
exploration consisted of site reconnaissance and limited drilling exploration along the
proposed highway alignment Results of our preliminary geotechnical engineering
exploration are presented in the report entitled Preliminary Geotechnical Engineering
Exploration Preliminary Engineering Environmental Assessment Kaumualii Highway
Widening Lihue to West of Maluhia Road Island of Kauai Hawaii dated
The borings drilled and laboratory tests performed for the
preliminary engineering study are presented in Appendix C and Appendix D respectively
for ease of reference
1 2 Project Considerations
The proposed project consists of widening the existing Kaumualii Highway from
the Town of Lihue extending approximately 7 3 miles west on the Island of Kauai
Hawaii The Phase 1A portion of the project will extend approximately 2 1 miles
westward from the Town of Lihue approximate Station No 384 60 to the west

1 3 Purpose and Scope
The purpose of our geotechnical engineering exploration was to obtain an overview
of the surface and subsurface conditions to develop a generalized soil and or rock data set
to formulate geotechnical recommendations for the design of grading foundations
retaining structures including specialty retaining structures and pavements for the
highway widening project In order to accomplish this we conducted an exploration
program generally consisting of the following tasks and work efforts
1 Review of the available in house soils and boring data in the project vicinity
obtained from the preliminary geotechnical engineering exploration
2 Performance of a surface reconnaissance of the proposed project alignment
and observing and mapping the existing conditions of surface soil and or
rock exposures

SECTION 2 0 SITE CHARACTERIZATION
2 1 Regional Geology
The Island of Kauai is composed of a single basalt shield volcano built by the
extrusion of lava of the Waimea Canyon Volcanic Series during the late Pliocene Epoch
more than 2 million years before present Following the cessation of this main shield
building phase there was renewed volcanic activity with the extrusion of basaltic lava of
the post erosional Koloa Volcanic Series and the concurrent deposition of the alluvial
sediments of the Palikea Formation
During the Pleistocene Epoch many sea level changes occurred as a result of
widespread continental glaciations As the great continental glaciers accumulated the
level of the ocean fell since there was less water available to fill the oceanic basins
Conversely as the glaciers receded or melted global sea levels rose because more
water was available The land mass of Kauai remained essentially stable during these
changes and the fluctuations were eustatic in nature These glacio eustatic fluctuations
resulted in stands of the sea that were both higher and lower relative to the present sea
level of Kauai
Most of the soils in the project area are composed of residual and saprolitic soils
derived from the in situ weathering of the Koloa Volcanic Series igneous rocks In
general residual and saprolitic soils grade to weathered basalt formations with increasing
depth Rocks of the Koloa Volcanic Series are generally characterized as thick flows of
dense basalt extruded from groups of vents aligned in north south trends in various
locales Associated with the vents are pyroclastic materials which usually form low
cinder cones at the vent Rocks of the Koloa Volcanic Series cover most of the eastern
half of the Island of Kauai including the majority of the proposed alignment
The highway alignment traverses Nawiliwili Stream and many smaller
drainageways and gullies Alluvial soil deposits of the Quaternary Period in age underlie
some portions of the project alignment The areas near Nawiliwili Stream contain
extensive deposits of alluvial sediments These sediments generally are unconsolidated
W O 3869 20 A GEOLABS INC Page 7
Hawaii California
SECTION 2 SITE CHARACTERIZATION
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
to moderately consolidated non calcareous soils deposited by erosion of the upper Koloa
Volcanic Series Smaller drainageways and gullies have also deposited alluvial sediments
in localized areas Agricultural and commercial developments within the last century have
brought the project site to its present conditions
2 2 General Site Conditions
The proposed Phase 1A alignment stretches from the vicinity of Anonui Street to
the Town of Lihue on the Island of Kauai Hawaii The project location and general vicinity
are shown on the Project Location Map Plate 1 The proposed new four lane Kaumuailii
Highway will stretch approximately 2 1 miles as shown on the General Site Plan Plate 2
The existing Kaumualii Highway is a two lane undivided highway It is the main
highway connecting Lihue with the other towns in the south central and southwestern
portions of Kauai such as Kalaheo Hanapepe and Waimea Based on our field
reconnaissance the existing pavement generally appeared to be in good condition The
eastbound traffic into the Town of Lihue during the morning rush hours was generally
moderate to heavy at the time of our site reconnaissance and field exploration We
observed a long line of cars traveling into Lihue on several occasions during the morning
hours with traffic slowly moving or sometimes reaching a complete standstill
2 3 Terrain and Vegetation
The terrain along the highway widening project limits varies significantly In
general the terrain along the existing highway may be classified into the following three
major groups
e developed and landscaped
e agricultural
e unused native lands
Most of the developed areas are east of Puhi Road toward the Town of Lihue In
this area there are several one and two story buildings close to the existing highway
Grass lawn and low lying weeds are typically found in landscaped areas along the existing
highway There are several open grass lawn areas fronting Kukui Grove Shopping
W O 3869 20 A GEOLABS INC Page 8
Hawaii California
SECTION 2 SITE CHARACTERIZATION
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
Center Kauai Community College and other localized areas The existing grades are
generally level to slightly sloping
The majority of the undeveloped land to the west of Puhi Road is used for
agricultural purposes Sugar cane was formerly cultivated at many of these locations
Where present sugar canes are typically up to 6 feet in height and moderate to heavy in
density Native grasses of up to about 4 feet in height were also observed in areas not
actively cultivated The terrain in most of the agricultural areas is generally level to slightly
sloping
The existing Kaumualii Highway traverses several flowing streams and many
smaller gullies and drainageways In these areas with more severe terrain crops are
typically not cultivated and the land remains unused Therefore the vegetation around
these locations is generally thick with shrubs and trees The existing grades can be as
steep as one horizontal to one vertical 1H 1V in some areas based on visual
observations
2 4 Existing Cut and Fill Slopes
In addition to the above mentioned terrain there are many existing cut slopes along
the highway Many of the smaller cuts of 10 feet or less in height are generally cut at
near vertical slopes In areas west of the Phase 1A alignment several major cut slopes of
up to about 30 feet in height appear to exist at about a 2H 1V slope inclination In general
the cut slopes with inclinations of about 2H 1V or flatter appear to be in relatively good
condition
Based on our site reconnaissance other steeper slopes regardless of the slope
height appear to have experienced slight to moderate degrees of raveling and or localized
slope failure The friable nature of the materials was implied by the existing slope face
condition and accumulation of loose soils at the toe of the slope The cut slope materials
were observed to be in a moist condition with growth of moss and other vegetation on the
exposed surfaces helping to retain the soil structure and maintain slope stability
Hawaii California
SECTION 2 SITE CHARACTERIZATION
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
The existing fill slopes generally are found where the highway traverses gullies or
drainageways Typically culverts are buried under the highway to allow for drainage of
surface water Based on our site reconnaissance fill slope inclinations of up to about
by visual observation were present along the highway In some areas the
surfaces of the fill slopes were generally loose with little to no vegetation Raveling
appears to occur readily at these locations In addition we observed that sections of the
existing guardrails were deformed indicating possible downslope creep movement
2 5 Existing Structures
As mentioned previously the proposed Phase 1A highway alignment will traverse
three large culverts and the Lihue Mill Bridge The existing conditions of the structures
which are summarized in the subsequent subsections include the following
Drain Line No 50
The existing structure at Drain Line No 5 crossing is a parabolic arch culvert The
culvert is made of CRM and has a width of about 4 feet The invert of the existing
culvert is about 24 feet below the pavement grade at the south side of the highway
We understand the existing parabolic arch culvert will be abandoned and replaced
with a new 8 foot by 4 foot box culvert The new culvert will be located about
40 feet east of the existing culvert
Drain Line No 7
A 4 foot by 4 foot concrete box culvert is currently present at the Drain Line No 7
location The existing box culvert will remain in operation and will be extended
through the widened portion of the new highway
Drain Line No 8
The existing structure at Drain Line No 8 is a CMP arch culvert The dimensions of
the existing culvert are about 7 feet 5 inches in width and 5 feet 5 inches in height
The existing culvert will be removed and replaced with a new 8 foot by 6 foot
concrete box culvert The new culvert will be located approximately at the same
location as the existing structure

Hawaii California

Lihue Mill Bridge
The existing Lihue Mill Bridge is a six span bridge founded on spread footings and
drilled shafts The total span length of about 309 feet traverses Nawiliwili Stream
and the sugar mill operations Both abutments and Bent No 5 at the western end
of the bridge are founded on spread footings An outcrop of very stiff
saprolite weathered basalt formation was observed near Bent No 5 The as built
plans indicated that the other four bents are supported by 51 inch diameter drilled
shafts Based on available information the drilled shafts are about 45 feet deep
with a tip elevation of about 129 feet MSL
2 6 Climate and Rainfall
Based on available information the mean annual temperature around the project
site is approximately 72 to 74 degrees Fahrenheit Temperatures are generally uniform
throughout the year with summer months slightly warmer than the winter months Annual
rainfalls of about 50 to 80 inches per year are recorded at weather stations near the
project site The rainfalls are most abundant from November through March when rainfalls
of over 10 inches per month may be encountered
Seepage of free water was not observed in the exposed road cuts during our field
exploration however the soil moisture conditions were observed to be very high Due to
the high rainfall of the area groundwater may be encountered as relatively shallow
perched layers especially near streams along the proposed alignment
2 7 Geologic Terms
Based on our evaluation of the geologic information obtained from the
subsurface exploration and to facilitate discussion of the geologic materials encountered
at the site we have classified the materials encountered into idealized categories of
earth materials and are used in the logs of borings presented in Appendix A The
idealized categories of earth materials are as follows

Fill Materials
Alluvium
Residual Soils and Saprolite
Basalt Formation
Fill Materials
This geologic material category includes man made earth fills that generally are
encountered as surface soil deposits in developed areas The various fill
materials encountered at the project site generally range from unconsolidated
deposits of medium stiff to very stiff clays and silts The fills may have been
placed as controlled fill during site grading or as undocumented deposits of
stockpile or backfill It should be noted that occasional large sized man made
debris and or rock fragments such as cobbles and boulders should be expected
within the surface fill materials
Alluvium
This geologic material category generally consists of unconsolidated soft to stiff
gray brown and black colored clays and silts with loose sands and gravel that
were transported and deposited by the action of moving water stream flow
Alluvium may contain marsh deposits consisting of dark colored organic clays
silts and sands that were deposited in a shallow water or marsh environment
These deposits are typically very soft and compressible
Alluvium may also contain peat and traces of relict shell or coralline material that
is generally indicative of a shallow marine depositional environment Alluvium
may also contain some occasionally hard cobbles and boulders or decomposed
rock fragments derived from erosion and transportation by stream flow The rock
fragments typically are rounded or sub rounded in shape as a result of the
abrasion experienced in the stream channel environment The alluvium
encountered at the project site typically represents buried stream channels
lagoons and marsh depositional environments that are mainly in valleys and
other low lying regions
Hawaii California
SECTION 2 SITE CHARACTERIZATION
2 8
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
Residual Soils and Saprolite
This category of geologic material contains a broad range of in situ weathered
basalt rock Extremely weathered rock is referred to as saprolitic material and
represents rock that has been reduced by weathering to soil like components
with decomposed rock fragments However the material retains the remnant
rock texture such as layering vesicularity and some fracture patterns
Saprolitic materials commonly are mottled in coloration and contain more sandy
and gravelly components with zones of less weathered rock contained within
Completely weathered rock is referred to as a residual soil and has lost all visible
rock texture characteristics Residual soils commonly are composed of clayey
and silty components of uniform coloration Some relict boulders of hard rock
occasionally may be encountered in residual soils
Basalt Formation
This category of geologic material contains volcanic basalt rock The character
of the basalt formation may be highly variable with depth because the lava was
generally deposited in various layers The basalt rock and interbeds of clinker
range from soft rock to very hard rock The basalt formation may range from
highly weathered to relatively unweathered in character

underlain by basalt formation at greater depths Due to the linear extent of the project
limits and variations in topographic and geologic features depths to basalt formation
vary significantly across the highway alignment from about 20 to 57 feet below the
existing ground surface Based on our field exploration the basalt formations
encountered generally range from extremely weathered to relatively unweathered and

such as the cut slopes along Hoomana Road steepened cut slopes with slope
stabilization and special erosion control measures may be incorporated into the design

FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
Based on the information provided new box culvert installation will require
placement of about 12 to 18 feet of new embankment fills on top of the culverts to achieve
design grades Because placement of new fills on soft ground conditions will cause
ground settlements we recommend over excavating the soft soils to expose firm
unyielding ground under the new box culvert crossings The over excavated soil should
be properly disposed off site and replaced with compacted select granular fill prior to new
embankment fill placement and or pavement construction In general removal and
replacement should be implemented in other areas with limited amounts of soft soil
deposits such as at minor drainage crossings and at inverts of small pipe culverts where
appropriate
Ground settlements are anticipated when new fills are placed over the soft ground
conditions and or areal fill is placed to raise the site to the proposed finished elevations In
order to reduce the effects of the anticipated settlements on the pavements structures
and other improvements constructed on the fills we recommend implementing a
settlement waiting period in selected locations after placement of the fills and prior to
construction of improvements on the fills
Our preliminary analyses indicate that the settlement waiting period may range
from a minimum of 1 month to about 6 months depending on the fill thickness foundation
soils and implementation of soft soil stabilization measures Because it is difficult to
accurately predict the exact time required for the filled ground to settle we recommend
implementing a settlement monitoring program using settlement gauges and settlement
markers Therefore provisions should be made for potential delays in the construction
schedule where a settlement waiting period is required
A new three span bridge and additions to the existing Lihue Mill Bridge will be
constructed for the project Based on the subsurface conditions encountered in the
borings scour considerations and the anticipated structural load demands of the bridge
structure we recommend supporting the abutment and pier structures of the new bridge
and existing bridge additions on a deep foundation system consisting of cast in place
concrete drilled shafts The drilled shafts should have a diameter of 5 feet and extend to

Hawaii California

depths ranging from about 65 to 105 feet below the bottoms of foundation caps A

Considerations
Based on our site reconnaissance and field exploration several types of
geotechnical considerations may have the potential for adverse effects on the stability of
the planned structures as well as the construction and future maintenance of the
proposed highway The geotechnical considerations observed in the field may include
but are not limited to the following
slope raveling or failure
settlement due to the soft soil conditions
seepage of groundwater
flooding associated with high intensity rainfall events
shoring and dewatering requirements during construction
Slope Raveling or Failure
Based on our observation of the topographic terrain across the project site we
anticipate the earthwork for the proposed highway project will involve cuts at
various locations throughout the project site We understand that substantial cuts
of up to about 40 feet deep in the residual soils and saprolite are planned In
addition fill embankments of up to about 18 feet high will be required at some
areas of the Phase 1A alignment During our site reconnaissance slope raveling
and localized slope failures of some cut slopes were observed Most of these slope
raveling and failures appeared to occur in relatively steep cut slopes with slope
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
inclinations of about 1H 1V or steeper Because of these existing conditions
implementation of an adequate erosion control program would be critical to the
satisfactory performance of the planned cut slopes and fill embankments to reduce
the potential for slope failure and or raveling
In general erosion of the existing slopes planned cut slopes or new fill
embankments are caused by surface water runoff General guidelines such as
establishment of vegetation and provision of drainage swales and earth berms are
provided and discussed in the Cut Slope Design and Fill Slope Design
subsections However we understand that due to the inherent properties of the
on site soils establishment of vegetation at the slope face may be difficult and may
require a long time Therefore other erosion control methods or applications such
as flexible geosynthetic matting should be considered as part of the overall erosion
control program for the proposed highway project
Flexible geosynthetic matting provides a flexible ground armor that resists erosion
while allowing natural vegetation to establish Flexible geosynthetic matting is
generally effective for temporary erosion control mulching permanent erosion
control and reduced surface water runoff velocity
Settlement Due to Soft Soils
Areas underlain by soft soil deposits are susceptible to consolidation and
settlement over time as man made fills are placed over these soft ground areas
Based on our field exploration and site reconnaissance soft soils likely will be
encountered at stream crossings drainageways and other localized areas The
effects of ground settlement due to the presence of soft soils are covered under the
Soft Ground Stabilization and Ground Settlement sections
Seepage of Groundwater
Based on our subsurface exploration at the project site the weathered soil
materials were found to have relatively high moisture contents Groundwater levels
observed in some of the borings drilled indicate that perched groundwater
W O 3869 20 A GEOLABS INC Page 20
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
conditions may exist in some areas Because of the high moisture found in the
weathered materials earthwork cuts may expose permeable layers responsible for
transmitting seepage of subsurface groundwater which in turn may cause
potentially unstable cut faces Therefore provisions should be made in the
construction documents to provide for subsurface drainage at select areas in the
field during construction
In addition relatively high in situ moisture content may create difficult conditions
during fill placement and compaction Therefore some moisture reduction may be
required to achieve the minimum compaction criteria especially for materials
primarily consisting of silts and clays Aeration to lower the soil moisture and more
compaction effort to achieve the specified compaction generally would reduce the
rate of fill placement for this project In addition adequate stockpile areas may not
be readily available on site
Flooding by Rainfall
Based on a review of available topographic maps and reconnaissance of the site
the Island of Kauai contains numerous rivers streams and drainage paths as a
result of the high volume of tropical rainfall Extended periods of heavy rainfall may
swell drainage pathways to their capacity Based on our site reconnaissance
many of the existing culvert crossings and bridges along the Kaumualii Highway are
partially choked with alluvial sediments and or debris Therefore the drainage
structures may not have the capacity to handle extreme storm events We wish to
emphasize that adequate modeling of the storm water runoff and scour potential
should be performed for the design of the proposed culvert and bridge structures
Shoring and Dewatering
We anticipate some relatively deep excavations will be required for construction of
some of the drainage structures and the Lihue Mill Bridge foundation caps
Open cut excavations may be desirable provided that the excavation may be set
back away from the existing on grade and below grade structures and utility lines
W O 3869 20 A GEOLABS INC Page 21
Hawaii California

FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
However the deep excavations will require installation of shoring systems such as
cofferdams and or steel sheet pile shoring
We envision that some of the excavations will be made in areas where the
groundwater table is at or near the existing ground surface Dewatering of the
excavations will be necessary where the planned excavation will extend below the
existing groundwater level Therefore dewatering provisions should be considered
for this project Because dewatering of the excavations will involve discharge of
groundwater a National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System NPDES permit
likely will be necessary
3 2 Site Grading
Based on the site topography along the general highway alignment we anticipate
that the majority of the cuts and fills will be on the order of about 10 feet high However
substantial cuts of up to about 40 feet deep may be anticipated at some locations such
as along Hoomana Road In addition some fills of up to about 18 feet thick may be
needed in some areas such as where the proposed highway traverses Drain Line No 5
In general grading work should conform to the Hawaii Standard Specifications
HSS for Road and Bridge Construction 2005 and the site specific recommendations
contained in this report Items of site grading that are addressed in the subsequent

Cut Slope Design
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered along the proposed alignment we
believe that the planned cut slopes likely will expose materials ranging from
medium stiff to very stiff residual soils and saprolite In general we believe that a
cut slope inclination of 2H 1V or flatter may be used for the design of the planned
cut slopes along the proposed alignment Where steeper cut slope inclinations are
desired such as the cut slopes along Hoomana Road steepened cut slopes with
slope stabilization and special erosion control measures may be incorporated into
the design Details of the steepened cut slopes with special erosion control
measures may be found in the Steepened Cut Slopes section herein
In the event that minor over cutting of a slope occurs or the cut slope is slightly
damaged by erosion consideration should be given to leaving the slope as is rather
than attempting to backfill the slope to the design grade with sliver fills Sliver fills
typically are highly susceptible to erosion and may cause raveling of the slope If
backfilling of an over cut slope is necessary the cut slope should be completely
over cut and buttressed with fill slope constructed of compacted fills by keying and
benching the fill materials into the over cut slope
Water should be diverted away from the tops of cut slopes Excessive surface
water runoff over the slope face may cause erosion of the exposed soils thus
jeopardizing the long term stability and performance of the cut slopes Therefore
we believe cut slopes should be protected by appropriate slope planting or by other
means such as placement of geotextile fabrics on the slope face as soon as
practical after the slope is excavated to reduce the potential for significant erosion
of the exposed soils
For cut slopes steeper than 3H 1V and vertical heights greater than 40 feet we
recommend that a minimum 8 foot wide bench be provided at the mid point of the
slope to reduce the potential for significant erosion due to surface water runoff
Lined drainage ditches also should be provided on the benches to collect and divert
surface water on the slopes to appropriate discharge outlets In addition V ditches
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
may be considered at the top and bottom of the cut slopes to provide proper
drainage of surface water runoff
Fill Slope Design
In general permanent embankments constructed of the compacted on site soils
placed over competent ground should be designed with a slope inclination of 2H 1V
or flatter Where soft soils are expected or encountered at the fill slope subgrade
appropriate soft soil stabilization measures should be implemented as discussed in
Soft Ground Stabilization section herein
Fills to be placed on existing slopes with inclinations steeper than 5H 1V should be
keyed and benched into the existing slope to provide stability of the new fill against
sliding For fill slopes with a vertical height greater than 10 feet we recommend
that a key be provided at the toe of the fill slope to provide stability of the new
embankment fill against sliding The key should be embedded at least 2 feet below
the lowest adjacent grade and should have a minimum base width of 10 feet
Keying and Benching Detail is provided on Plate 4
The filling operations should start at the lowest point and continue up in level
horizontal compacted layers Fill slopes should be constructed by overfilling and
cutting back to the design slope ratio to obtain a well compacted slope face
Water should be diverted away from the tops of fill slopes and slope planting
should be provided as soon as the final slope is completed to reduce the potential
for significant erosion of the finished slopes In addition we recommend that a
minimum 8 foot wide bench be provided at the mid point of the slope for fill slopes
steeper than 3H 1V and greater than 40 feet in vertical height to reduce the
potential for significant erosion due to surface water runoff Construction of earth
berms V ditches and the use of geotextile fabrics over the fill slope face should be
considered to reduce the potential for erosion thus enhancing the long term
stability of the planned cut and fill slopes
Hawaii California

Site Preparation
At the on set of earthwork areas within the contract grading limits should be
cleared and grubbed thoroughly It should be noted that portions of the existing
terrain are heavily vegetated Vegetation debris deleterious materials and other
unsuitable materials should be removed and disposed of properly off site to reduce
the potential for contamination of the excavated materials
Soft and yielding areas encountered during clearing and grubbing below areas
designated to receive fill should be over excavated to expose firm natural material
and the resulting excavation should be backfilled with well compacted general fill
The excavated soft soils should be properly disposed of off site
In general the over excavated subgrades and areas designated to receive fills
exposing soils should be scarified to a depth of about 8 _ inches
moisture conditioned to above the optimum moisture content and recompacted to
a minimum of 90 percent relative compaction
Fills and Backfills
In general fills and backfills required to raise the grade to the finished grades and
pavement subgrades may consist of compacted general fills In general the
near surface silty and clayey residual and saprolite soils encountered during our
field exploration should be suitable for use as general fill materials provided the
materials are free of vegetation organics deleterious materials and clay lumps
and rock fragments greater than 6 inches in maximum dimension The on site cut
materials generated from excavations into the underlying weathered basalt
formation may be used as general fill or backfill materials provided that they are
screened of the over sized materials and or processed to meet the above gradation
requirements less than 6 inches in largest dimension
Imported material to be used as select granular fill should be non expansive
granular material such as crushed coral mudrock basalt or cinder sand The
select granular fill should be well graded from coarse to fine with particles no larger
W O 3869 20 A GEOLABS INC Page 25
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
than 3 inches in largest dimension The material should also contain less than
15 percent particles passing the No 200 sieve The material should have a
laboratory CBR value of 25 or more and should have a maximum swell value of
one percent or less Imported fill materials should be tested for conformance with
these recommendations prior to delivery to the project site for the intended use
We anticipate that some areas of the project may encounter groundwater _ If
groundwater is encountered fill materials should consist of free draining granular
materials such as AASHTO M 43 No 67 gradation wrapped on all sides with
non woven geotextile fabric Section 716 02 of HSS The free draining granular
materials should be used up to a level about 12 inches above the groundwater level
to facilitate compaction of the fill materials
Fill Placement and Compaction Requirements
In general fills and backfills should be moisture conditioned to above the optimum
moisture content placed in level lifts not exceeding 8 inches in loose thickness and
compacted to at least 90 percent relative compaction Fills and backfills within
3 feet of the pavement grade elevation should be compacted to a minimum of
95 percent relative compaction Relative compaction refers to the in place dry
density of soil expressed as a percentage of the maximum dry density of the same
soil determined in accordance with AASHTO T 180 Optimum moisture is the
water content percentage by dry weight corresponding to the maximum dry
density Compaction should be accomplished by sheepsfoot rollers vibratory
rollers or other types of acceptable compaction equipment Water tamping jetting
or ponding should not be allowed to compact the fills
It should be noted that some of the on site soils generally exist in a relatively moist
to wet condition Therefore some moisture reduction may be required to achieve
the minimum 90 percent compaction criteria especially for materials primarily
consisting of silts and clays Aeration to lower the soil moisture and more
compaction effort to achieve the specified compaction would generally reduce the
rate of fill placement for this project In addition adequate stockpile areas may not
Hawaii California

be readily available on site Contractors proposing to work on this project should be

Excavations
Our site reconnaissance and field exploration program generally encountered
alluvium residual soils and saprolite consisting of medium stiff to very stiff clayey
silts near the ground surface Basalt rock formation may be encountered in deeper
excavations and in localized areas along the project alignment In general it is our
opinion that conventional heavy excavation equipment such as a large bulldozer
excavator or similar heavy construction equipment may achieve the excavations in
alluvium residual soils and saprolite However excavations into the harder basalt
formation will likely require the use of hoerams or chipping

should comply with all applicable local safety requirements The above discussions
regarding the rippability of the surface materials are based on field data obtained
from our field reconnaissance and the borings performed at the project site
Contractors proposing to work on this project should be encouraged to examine the
site conditions to make their own interpretation

alignment will traverse soft and or unstable grounds such as at drainageways and stream
crossings There are several ways to mitigate potential instability and ground settlements
associated with soft soils

are discussed below The items of soft soil stabilization that are addressed in the
subsequent subsections include the following

Remove and Replace
Based on our field exploration the soft soils encountered in the borings drilled
within the limits of Phase 1A generally are near culvert crossings and drainageways
except near Lihue Mill Bridge The depths at which soft soils were encountered in
the borings were generally near the ground surface or inverts of culvert crossings
Based on the information provided the new box culvert installation will require
placement of about 12 to 18 feet of new embankment fills on top of the culverts to
achieve design grades Because placement of new fills over soft ground conditions
will cause ground settlements we recommend over excavating the soft soils to
expose firm unyielding ground under the new box culvert crossings The
over excavated soil should be properly disposed off site and replaced with
free draining granular fill such as AASHTO M 43 No 57 or No 67 gradations
prior to new embankment fill placement and or pavement construction The sides
of the excavation including shored excavation should be lined with a non woven
geotextile fabric such as Mirafi 180N or equivalent for separation purposes prior to
placement of the free draining granular fill A Typical Box Culvert Over Excavation

Blanket Stabilization
Because the surface soils in the areas surrounding drainage crossings would be
marshy and wet we believe it may not have sufficient strength to support the
weight of heavy construction equipment In addition difficulties may be
encountered in placement of additional fills due to the generally moist ground
conditions Therefore a working platform or drainage blanket should be
established prior to filling in these areas The working platform may consist of a
combination of a woven geotextile fabric and a gravel layer The woven geotextile
fabric Mirafi 600X or equivalent would reduce the need for strict gradation
requirements of the drainage blanket because it would act as a filter medium to
W O 3869 20 A GEOLABS INC Page 29
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
reduce the potential for gravel loss into the soft ground In addition the woven
geotextile also would reduce the potential for the mud and fine soil particles to
contaminate the granular drainage blanket material It also provides tensile
reinforcement to the gravel layer and permits earlier use of heavy equipment on a
minimum thickness of gravel
The drainage blanket should consist of granular crushed rock materials such as
Size 4 Coarse Aggregate of Section 703 05 of HSS and should be at least
30 inches thick A layer of non woven geotextile fabric or permeable separator
Section 716 02 of HSS should be provided between the crushed rock material
and the upper fill material
Due to the soft and yielding nature of the subsoils new fills should be placed in
relatively thin level lifts at slow filling rates to reduce the potential for shear failure
in the soft materials The controlled rate of filling should be maintained to allow the
consolidating soils to gain sufficient strength to support the next lift The actual
filling rate in the soft areas should be established in the field based on settlement
gauge readings and adjusted to meet field conditions
3 4 Ground Settlement
Ground settlements are anticipated when new fills are placed over the existing
ground to raise the site to the proposed finished elevations Significant ground settlement
likely will occur when new fills are placed over soft ground conditions
In order to reduce the effects of the anticipated settlements on the pavements
structures and other improvements constructed on the fills implementation of a
settlement waiting period will be necessary after placement of the fills and prior to
construction of improvements on the fills
Our preliminary analyses indicate that the settlhement waiting period may range
from a minimum of 1 month to about 6 months depending on the fill thickness foundation
soils and implementation of soft soil stabilization measures Because it is difficult to
accurately predict the exact time required for the filled ground to settle a settlement

Settlement Waiting Period and Settlement Monitoring
Our site reconnaissance indicated that areas around many of the existing culvert
inlets and outlets are underlain by soft soil deposits Because of the potential for
significant soft ground settlements under the new fill loads consideration should
be given to filling these areas first for surcharging purposes with a temporary
culvert for drainage Construction of the permanent drainage structures in these
soft ground areas should be delayed until a majority of the anticipated settlements
has occurred After a large portion of the estimated settlements has occurred the
filled area may then be excavated and construction of the permanent drainage
structures may then commence
Even with the implementation of a settlement monitoring program some amount of
residual settlement should still be expected after construction of the permanent
drainage structure Therefore we recommend consideration be given to
over sizing the drainage structures to account for the loss in hydraulic capacity due
long term settlement of the drainage structures especially where permanent
drainage structures are located in the soft soil areas
It should be recognized that it is difficult to accurately predict the exact time
required for the filled ground to settle because the settlement rates are affected by
variations of the subsoil structure and the history of the subsoil deposition For the
very soft alluvial deposits with a substantial clay component we believe that the
estimated settlement period could vary by as much as 50 to 100 percent from the
actual settlement period Therefore the actual settlement rates should be
Hawaii California

monitored and a settlement monitoring program should be established to evaluate
the magnitude and rate of the estimated settlements during construction prior to
construction of the pavement structure and other improvements on the fills In
addition provisions should be made for potential delays in the construction
schedule if a longer settlement period is required
To monitor the actual settlement rate we recommend installing settlement gauges
and settlement markers in areas where fills are placed over soft soils Settlement

In general the gauges or markers should be read optically by a qualified

Temporary Surcharge Fills
As indicated previously ground settlements due to the placement of fills over soft
compressible deposits are anticipated The settlement rates for the soft
compressible deposits would be much slower and would require longer settlement
waiting periods to reduce the effects of settlement on the improvements
constructed on the fills The settlement waiting period for fill embankments
constructed over compressible deposits may take months to years depending on
the nature and thickness of the compressible deposit
In order to reduce the settlement waiting period and subsequent long term
settlements a temporary surcharge program may be considered for the fill
embankment over soft soil areas Surcharge programs can aid in accelerating
ground settlements thus reducing the settlement waiting period However it
should be noted that potential slope instability of the fill embankment may be
induced as a result of improperly designed temporary surcharge fills
Depending on the fill thickness and the nature of the soft compressible deposit a
30 day waiting period generally is recommended between completion of the
general fill and the temporary surcharge fill placement This waiting period is
required to allow dissipation of excess pore pressures in order for the subsoils to
gain strength and to reduce excessive lateral deformation and possible failure
within the soft soils A slope ratio of up to may be used for the temporary
surcharge fill material on top of the fill The surcharge fill may be placed in lifts of
3 feet high lightly compacted after the end of the 30 day waiting period Adequate
precautions must be taken to reduce erosion of the surcharge fill slopes during
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
construction and the subsequent surcharge period to attain the required
consolidation The actual settlement waiting period with the temporary surcharge
fill will be determined by settlement gauge or marker readings as described in the
previous subsection
3 5 Drilled Shaft Foundations
A new three span bridge will be constructed adjacent to the existing Lihue Mill
Bridge In addition a new bike lane and a sidewalk will be added to the existing Lihue
Mill Bridge Based on the subsurface conditions encountered in the borings scour
considerations and the anticipated structural load demands of the bridge structure we
recommend supporting the abutment and pier structures of the new bridge and existing
bridge additions by a deep foundation system consisting of cast in place concrete drilled
shafts
Based on the anticipated structural load demands we envision that the drilled
shaft foundations for the bridge structure will consist of 5 foot diameter drilled shafts In
general the cast in place concrete drilled shafts would derive vertical support primarily
from skin friction The end bearing component of the drilled shafts has been discounted
in our analysis due to difficulties associated with obtaining a clean bottom during
construction in the relatively deep drilled shafts Based on the information provided it
should be noted that the following assumptions were made and or provided for our use in
our foundation analyses
e Scour of the foundation materials at the abutment locations will not occur at
the project site
e Scour of the foundation materials at the pier locations may extend down to
Elevation 118 feet MSL

pier locations

Uplift Load Resistance
In general uplift loads may be resisted by a combination of the dead weight of the
drilled shaft and by shear along the shaft surface and the adjacent soils
Considering that the drilled shafts are designed based on adhesion between the
shaft and the surrounding soils recommendations pertaining to the uplift load
capacity for the extreme event and strength limit states are presented in the
following table The values provided below assume an effective uplift resistance
length of 40 feet for the drilled shafts

Foundation Settlements
Settlement of the drilled shaft foundation will result from elastic compression of the
shaft and subgrade response of the foundation embedded in the alluvium residual
soils saprolite and or basalt formations Total settlements of the drilled shafts
under the service limit state loading conditions are estimated to be on the order of
less than 0 5 inches Differential settlements between the abutments and the
intermediate piers are estimated to be less than 0 25 inches We believe that a
significant portion of the settlement is elastic and should occur as the loads are
applied Post construction foundation settlements should be less than 0 25 inches
Drilled Shaft Construction Considerations
In general the performance of drilled shafts depends significantly upon the
contractor s method of installation and construction procedures The load bearing
capacities of drilled shafts depend on the frictional resistance between the shaft
and the surrounding soil Therefore the contractor should exercise care in drilling
the shaft holes and in placing concrete into the holes
Based on our field exploration soft to very soft alluvium was encountered below the
surface fills Due to the loose and or soft consistency of these materials caving in
and or sloughing of these materials likely would occur during the drilling operations
To reduce the potential for caving in of the drilled holes temporary casing of the
drilled holes will be required during the drilled shaft installation However it should
be noted that the contractor may experience some difficulty in the removal of the
casing after completion of the concrete placement due to the subsurface conditions
at the project site In addition the anticipated shallow groundwater level at the
project site also may pose some construction difficulties because direct observation
of the sides and bottoms of the drilled shaft may not be possible
It should be noted that boulders and cobbles likely will be encountered in the fill
alluvium residual soils and or saprolite layers Therefore some difficult drilling
conditions may be encountered at the project site and should be expected The
drilled shaft contractor will need to have the appropriate equipment and tools to drill
W O 3869 20 A GEOLABS INC Page 40
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
through these types of natural obstructions where encountered in the subsurface
Appropriate measures also will be needed to avoid dislodging boulders into the
drilled shaft hole during the drilling and shaft installation process
In addition the drilled shafts will be embedded into the basalt formation
encountered at greater depths Therefore coring into the dense basalt formations
encountered in the borings will be required The drilled shaft contractor will need to
demonstrate that the proposed drilling equipment and coring tools will be able to
install the drilled shafts to the recommended depths and dimensions by
performance of a trial shaft program
Workmanship
The load carrying capacities of the drilled shafts depend to a large extent on the
contact between the drilled shafts and the surrounding materials Therefore proper
construction techniques are important The contractor should exercise care in
drilling the shaft holes in rifling the sides of the drilled shaft holes and in placing
concrete into the holes
Due to the presence of groundwater at relatively shallow depths concrete
placement by tremie methods will be required during construction of the drilled
shafts The concrete should be placed promptly after the completion of drilling
within 24 hours to reduce the potential for caving in and or softening of the
sidewalls The concrete should be placed in a suitable manner by displacing the
water in an upward fashion from the bottom of the drilled hole A low shrink
concrete mix with high slump 7 to 9 inch slump range should be used to provide
close contact between the drilled shafts and the surrounding soils The concrete
should be placed in a suitable manner to reduce the potential for segregation of
the aggregates from the concrete mix
In consideration of the difficulties of the subsurface conditions at the site and the
complexity of the drilled shaft foundation for this project we recommend that the
drilled shaft contractor for this project be pre qualified during the bidding process
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
Trial Shaft Program
A trial shaft program is normally required and highly recommended for bridge
projects Considering the diameters and structural load capacities of the drilled
shafts for this project we recommend conducting a trial shaft program to fulfill the
following objectives
e To examine the adequacy of the methods and equipment proposed
by the contractor to install the drilled shafts into the conglomerate and
basalt formation
e To assess the contractor s method of rifling the sidewalls of the drilled
shaft holes prior to placing tremie concrete
e To assess the contractor s method of placing the permanent casing
for the drilled shaft
e To assess the contractor s method of tremie concrete placement
To achieve these objectives we recommend that the trial shaft program consist of
drilling one 5 foot diameter sacrificial trial shaft The location of the trial shaft
should be near but outside of the pier foundation locations We anticipate that the
trial shaft will be drilled from the existing ground surface prior to excavation of the
pier footings Therefore in order for the trial shafts to extend to near the production
shaft tip elevations the trial shaft should have a minimum length of about 110 feet
After drilling the trial shafts the trial shafts should be backfilled with unreinforced
concrete in the same manner that the production shafts are to be constructed
We anticipate that temporary casing will likely be required during the trial shaft
installation to reduce the potential for caving in of the drilled holes Therefore we
recommend that a Geolabs representative be present during the trial shaft program
to evaluate the contractors method of drilled shaft installation and to evaluate the
subsurface materials encountered

As part of the pre construction activities we recommend conducting two static load
tests on 5 foot diameter concrete drilled shafts The load test results will be used to

confirm or modify the estimated tip elevations of the production shafts Due to the

In general we recommend the load test shafts be structurally reinforced and
instrumented with vibrating wire embedment strain gauges for load testing
purposes As a minimum two embedment strain gauges should be placed at each
level starting from the bottom at an elevation of about 5 feet above and below the
load cells and subsequently at about 8 foot intervals A schematic sketch showing
the recommended instrumentation of the load test shaft is provided on the Drilled

Due to the relatively high capacities recommended for the drilled shafts a
conventional load test would not be practical and would be costly to conduct
Therefore we recommend conducting bi directional axial load tests using an
expandable load cell Osterberg Load Cell The bi directional load test separately
tests the shear resistance and end bearing components of the drilled shaft by
loading the shaft in two directions upward for shear resistance and downward for
end bearing and shear resistance
The Osterberg Load Cells should have a minimum diameter of 26 inches and
should be capable of applying a load of 3 600 kips in each direction The
expandable base load cell will need to be attached to the reinforcing cage of the
load test shaft prior to lowering the cage in place as shown on Plate 6
The drilled shaft load test should be performed in general accordance with the
Quick Load Test Method of ASTM D 1143 The load test shaft should be loaded to
failure to evaluate the ultimate side shear resistance of the shaft Installation of the
expandable load cells installation of the embedment strain gauges performance of
the bi directional axial load tests and presentation of the load test data should be
performed by a professional experienced in these types of load testing procedures
The load test shafts should be loaded at increments of about 100 to 150 kips and

Non Destructive Integrity Testing
Based on the critical nature of the drilled shaft foundations for the new bridge
structure we recommend that non destructive integrity testing be conducted on
each production drilled shafts for the project One of the non destructive integrity
testing methods such as Crosshole Sonic Logging CSL has been gaining
widespread use and acceptance for integrity testing of drilled shafts
Crosshole Sonic Logging techniques are based on the propagation of sound waves
through concrete In general the actual velocity of sound wave propagation in
concrete is dependent on the concrete material properties geometry of the element
and wave length of the sound waves When ultrasonic frequencies are generated
Pressure P waves and Shear S waves travel though the concrete If anomalies
are contained in the concrete the anomalies will reduce the P wave travel velocity
in the concrete Anomalies in the drilled shaft concrete may include soil particles
gravel water voids contaminated concrete and highly segregated constituent
particles
The transit time of an ultrasonic P wave signal may be measured between an
ultrasonic transmitter and receiver in two parallel water filled access tubes placed
into the concrete during construction The P wave velocity can be obtained by
dividing the measured transit time from the distance between the transmitter and
receiver Therefore anomalies may be detected if they exist
W O 3869 20 A GEOLABS INC Page 44
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
To reduce the potential for de bonding between the access tube and the
surrounding concrete we recommend the access tubes consist of standard steel
pipe with a minimum inside diameter of 2 inches In addition the access tube
should be equipped with watertight couplings In general the access tubes should
be securely attached to the interior of the reinforcing cage as near to parallel as
possible in the drilled shaft We recommend casting a minimum of five access
tubes into the concrete of the 5 foot diameter drilled shafts Details pertaining to
the configuration of the access tubes for crosshole sonic logging tests are
presented on Plate 6
In addition the access tubes should extend from the bottom of the drilled shaft
reinforcing cage to at least 3 5 feet above the top of the shaft The bottom of the
access tube should be permanently capped It is imperative that joints required to
achieve the full length of the access tubes be watertight It is the responsibility of
the contractor to take extra care to prevent damaging the access tubes during the
placement of the reinforcing cage into the drilled hole The tubes should be filled
with potable water as soon as possible but not later than 4 hours after the
concrete placement Subsequently the top of the access tubes should be capped
with watertight caps
The Crosshole Sonic Logging CSL test of drilled shafts should be conducted
after at least 7 days of curing time but no later than 21 days after concrete
placement In addition the CSL test of drilled shafts should be performed in
general accordance with ASTM D 6760 In the event that a drilled shaft is found to
have significant anomalies and or is suspected to be defective based on the CSL
testing and or field observations the drilled shaft should be cored to evaluate the
integrity of the concrete in the drilled shaft The coring location within the drilled
shaft should be determined by a Geolabs representative who should be present to
observe the installation of the drilled shafts After completion of the crosshole
sonic logging of the drilled shafts all the access tubes should be filled with grout of
the same strength as the drilled shaft concrete

As mentioned previously the actual velocity of sound wave propagation in
concrete is dependent on the concrete material properties geometry of the
element and wavelength of the sound waves Therefore the ultrasonic pulse
velocity through the actual concrete mix should be tested in general accordance
with ASTM C 597 In general we recommend performing a series of the
Ultrasonic Pulse Velocity measurements at 1 day 3 days 5 days 7 days and 9
days to establish a relationship of pulse velocity of concrete and age of concrete
for the actual concrete mix
Foundations
We understand that a new deck will be constructed for the existing Lihue Mill
Bridge In order to support the additional loads from the new deck new foundations will
need to be added to the existing bridge foundations Based on the subsurface conditions
encountered at the project site we recommend using a deep foundation system to support
the additional loads to the existing bridge Due to the limited space for new foundations
we believe that a deep foundation system consisting of micropiles may be used to provide
additional foundation support to the existing bridge structure
In general a micropile foundation system consists of a small diameter usually less
than 12 inches drilled and grouted pile with steel reinforcing The micropile foundation
typically is constructed by drilling a borehole placing reinforcing steel in the hole and
grouting the borehole Micropiles are desirable because they can be installed readily in
access restrictive environments and in numerous soil types and ground conditions In
addition installation of the micropiles generally causes minimal disturbance to adjacent
structures the adjacent soils and the environment
Based on experience we believe a micropile system with a minimum grout bulb
diameter of 7 5 inches may be used to support the proposed structure Based on the
subsurface conditions encountered at the project site and the available reinforcing bars
we recommend designing each micropile based on an allowable compressive load
capacity of 100 kips The allowable compressive load capacity for the micropiles is for
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
those caused by wind or seismic forces
We recommend extending the micropiles through the soft fill and alluvial soils and
bear on or in the stiff saprolite and basalt formation layers To provide an allowable
compressive load capacity of 50 tons 100 kips we believe that the micropiles may be
about 60 feet in length and embedded a minimum of 40 feet into the bearing layer
consisting of stiff saprolite and dense basalt formation The micropiles will derive its
vertical support primarily from skin friction between the grout bulb bonded zone and the
surrounding stiff saprolite and basalt formation
Based on the subsurface conditions at the project site we recommend using a
permanent casing at the upper portion of the micropile unbonded zone The permanent
casing should have an outside diameter OD of about 7 5 inches same as the grout bulb
size The permanent casing should extend to about Elevation 121 feet MSL about
20 feet of permanent casing below the foundation cap Based on the depth of the
permanent casing the micropile tip elevation would extend to Elevation 76 feet MSL In
general the micropiles should have a minimum center to center spacing of about
30 inches to avoid reduction in vertical load capacity due to group action and to facilitate
drilling of the micropiles The following contains the summary of the micropiles
The load supporting capacity of micropiles is highly dependent on the installation
procedures of the micropiles Therefore the actual production micropile lengths should be
established after completion of a load test program Due to possible variation in the length
of the micropiles unit prices should be obtained during bidding for add ons shorter
micropiles etc
Micropile Load Test Program
It should be noted that the compressive load capacity of the micropiles is highly
dependent on the drilling procedures and the grouting methods employed by the
contractor to install the micropile Therefore the compressive load capacity of the
micropile may vary considerably between different contractors and micropile
Hawaii California

FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
foundation systems In order to determine whether the contractor s methods of
micropile installation are adequate and to determine the ultimate compressive load
capacity we recommend performing at least one pre production compressive load
test on a sacrificial micropile In general the purpose of the pre production load
test on a sacrificial micropile is to fulfill the following objectives
To examine the adequacy of the methods and equipment proposed
by the contractor to install the micropiles to the depths required
To assess the contractor s method of drilling and grout injection
In general the pre production load test should be performed in accordance with
ASTM Test Designation D 1143 Based on experience we believe that the load
test should be conducted no earlier than 7 days after completion of the micropile
installation Two additional micropiles may be used for reaction during the
compressive load testing of the pre production load test micropile The reaction
micropiles may be installed to depths as deep as the load test micropile to provide
adequate reaction in uplift
The load test micropile should be loaded gradually to at least 200 percent of the
allowable design load in compression We recommend that the maximum test load
200 percent of the design load be held for a minimum of 8hours The
pre production load test is an integral part of the design of the micropile foundation
system Therefore we recommend conducting the pre production load test under
the observation of a representative from Geolabs
Lateral Load Resistance
It should be noted that the micropile foundation system provides low lateral load
resistance due to the relatively small diameter of the micropile Therefore the
lateral load resistance contributed by the micropiles should be neglected Lateral
loads imposed on the building addition may be resisted primarily by passive
pressure resistance acting against the vertical faces of the pile cap and grade
beams and by shear acting along the sides of the pile caps and grade beams
Passive pressure resistance against the vertical faces of the pile cap and grade
Hawaii California

beams may be estimated using an equivalent fluid pressure of 350 pounds per
square foot per foot of depth pcf and 175 pcf for extreme event and strength limit
states respectively
Micropile Foundation Settlements
Settlements of the micropile foundations will result primarily from elastic
compression of the pile member and subgrade response We estimate the total
settlement of the pile supported foundations to be less than 1 inch with differential
settlements between columns supported on micropiles not exceeding about
one half of the total settlement We believe that these settlements are essentially
elastic and should occur as the loads are applied
Micropile Construction Considerations
Installation of micropiles should be performed by a specialty subcontractor
experienced in the construction of a micropile foundation system Due to the
specialized nature of the micropile foundation construction observation of the
micropile foundation installation system and testing of the micropiles should be
designated a Special Inspection item Therefore observation of the micropile
installation operations by Geolabs is necessary to confirm our design assumptions
Conventional Retaining Structures
As mentioned previously Retaining Wall Nos 2 through 6 generally will consist
of a conventional concrete retaining wall Based on the information provided the
heights of the six retaining walls will range up to about 15 feet In general the following
guidelines may be used for design of conventional concrete retaining walls planned at the
project site Discussions on specialty retaining walls such as segmental retaining walls
are presented separately in subsequent sections of this report

Dynamic Lateral Earth Forces
Based on the LRFD method the effects of seismic activity will need to be
considered in the design of retaining structures For the Island of Kauai the
coefficient of seismic acceleration may be estimated at 0 09 A
Dynamic lateral earth forces due to seismic loading may be estimated
by using 3H pounds per lineal foot of wall length for level backfill conditions where
H is the height of the wall in feet For walls with a sloping backfill up to 2H 1V
slope inclination dynamic lateral forces due to seismic loading may be estimated
using 10H pounds per lineal foot of wall H in feet for an unrestrained wall
condition It should be noted that the dynamic lateral earth forces provided is in
addition to the static lateral earth pressures The resultant force should be
assumed to act through the mid height of the wall An appropriately reduced factor
of safety may be used when dynamic lateral earth forces are accounted for in the
design of the retaining structures
Drainage
Retaining walls should be well drained to reduce the build up of hydrostatic
pressures A typical drainage system should consist of permeable material such

as AASHTO M 43 No 67 gradation material placed near the bottom and along the

non woven geotextile fabric at each of the weephole locations The weepholes
should be spaced not more than 8 feet apart
Backfill behind the permeable drainage zone should consist of Type A Structure
Backfill Material conforming to Section 703 20 of the HSS or granular backfill
material with a maximum particle size of 3 inches Unless covered by concrete
slabs or pavements the upper 12 inches of backfill should consist of relatively
impervious material to reduce the potential for significant water infiltration behind
the walls In addition the backfill from the bottom of the wall to about the elevation
of the weepholes should consist of relatively impervious soil backfill such as
well compacted on site soils to reduce the potential for excess water infiltration
into the foundation materials
3 8 Mechanically Stabilized Earth MSE Walls
Due to the sloping terrain and relatively thick fills required up to about 18 feet we
recommend using a Mechanically Stabilized Earth MSE retaining wall system as an
alternate solution to the conventional concrete retaining wall supporting the major fill
embankments planned at new Retaining Wall No 1 The MSE retaining wall system is a
composite wall system which utilizes high density polyethylene or other reinforcing
elements to reinforce the backfill zone and to improve the shear strength of the reinforced
soil zone This composite system essentially forms a gravity wall structure with an ability
to tolerate significant total and differential settlements In addition MSE retaining walls
also are desirable due to the flexibility of the wall ease of construction high load carrying
capacity and economy
Design of the MSE retaining wall system will need to take into consideration both
the external and internal stability of the structure In evaluating external stability the
MSE retaining wall must satisfy four stability conditions 1 bearing failure
W O 3869 20 A GEOLABS INC Page 53
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
FINAL DESIGN SUBMITTAL
2 translational sliding 3 overturning stability and 4 overall slope stability
Geotechnical design parameters to evaluate these stability conditions are presented in
the following subsections Some of the geotechnical parameters necessary in
evaluating the internal stability of the MSE retaining wall are presented in the
Reinforced Fill and Backfill Materials subsection
MSE Retaining Wall Foundations
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered at the project site we recommend
using an ultimate bearing capacity of to evaluate the foundations bearing
on the on site clayey silts and or compacted aggregate subbase based on an
extreme event limit state The extreme event limit state of the foundations is
generally due to unique occurrences such as seismic loading conditions

state a bearing pressure of may be used In general the retaining wall

6 foot setback distance measured horizontally from the outside edge of the
foundation to the face of the slope for sloping ground conditions
The wall subgrades should be compacted to a minimum of 90 percent relative
compaction to provide a firm and unyielding base Soft and or loose soils
encountered at the wall subgrades should be over excavated to a minimum depth
of 24 inches below the bottom of wall elevation The over excavation also should
extend a minimum of 24 inches laterally beyond the front face of the walls The
resulting over excavation should be backfilled with aggregate subbase materials
Lateral loads acting on the structures may be resisted by friction between the

against the near vertical faces of the foundation system The following coefficient

Overall Slope Stability
Overall slope stability should be evaluated for the MSE wall structures planned for
the project The factor of safety for short term and long term stability of the MSE
retaining wall should be at least 1 3 and 1 5 respectively which is the minimum
factor of safety normally recommended In areas where adequate factor of safeties
are not met soft ground stabilization measures and or staged construction would
need to be implemented
In general a minimum base width to wall height ratio of at least 0 80 is
recommended for the MSE retaining wall structures planned at the project site
We also recommend that the base width be at least 8 feet to develop sufficient
length for overall stability
Reinforced Fill and Backfill Materials
We believe that the reinforced fill material for the MSE retaining wall should
consist of imported select granular fill materials In general the imported select
granular fill materials should be well graded from coarse to fine with particles no
larger than 3 inches in largest dimension The material should also contain less
than 15 percent particles passing the No 200 sieve The material should have a
California Bearing Ratio CBR value of 25 or higher and a swell potential of
one percent or less when tested in accordance with ASTM Test Designation
D 1883
In addition the reinforced fill material imported select granular fill materials
should have an angle of internal friction of at least 38 degrees when tested by the
standard direct shear test ASTM D 3080 The sample to be tested should be
compacted to 95 percent relative compaction at moisture contents above the
optimum
Hawaii California
SECTION 3 DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
